🤔 AI effective tips collection 🧠
❓:2. (This sub-question 20 points) Let a, b, c be non-negative real numbers, a+b+c=3,Find the maximum value of S=left(a^{2}-a b+b^{2}right)left(b^{2}-b c+c^{2}right)left(c^{2}-c a+a^{2}right)

🧠:[<think>Alright, so I have this problem here where I need to find the maximum value of S, which is the product of three expressions: (a² - ab + b²), (b² - bc + c²), and (c² - ca + a²). The constraints are that a, b, c are non-negative real numbers adding up to 3. Hmm, okay. Let's see how to approach this.First, since the variables are non-negative and their sum is fixed at 3, this is a constrained optimization problem. I remember that for optimization problems with symmetry, sometimes testing symmetric cases like when all variables are equal can give maximum or minimum values. But before jumping into that, maybe I should analyze the expression S more closely.Each term in the product S is of the form (x² - xy + y²). Wait, I recall that x² - xy + y² is always non-negative because it can be rewritten as (x - y/2)² + (3y²)/4, which is a sum of squares. So each term in the product is non-negative, which makes sense since a, b, c are non-negative. So S itself is non-negative.Now, since we need to maximize S, maybe we can consider cases where one or two variables are zero. For example, if we set c = 0, then the problem reduces to two variables with a + b = 3, and S becomes (a² - ab + b²)(b²)(a²). Similarly, if we set b = 0, then S becomes (a²)(c² - ca + a²)(c²). Maybe these edge cases can give us some insight.Let me try the case where one variable is zero. Let's set c = 0. Then a + b = 3, and S = (a² - ab + b²)(b²)(a²). Let's compute this. Let’s denote a + b = 3, so b = 3 - a. Then:First term: a² - ab + b² = a² - a(3 - a) + (3 - a)² = a² - 3a + a² + 9 - 6a + a² = 3a² - 9a + 9.Second term: b² = (3 - a)².Third term: a².So S = (3a² - 9a + 9)(3 - a)² a².This seems complicated, but maybe I can find its maximum by taking the derivative with respect to a. However, calculus might be messy here. Alternatively, maybe testing specific values can help. Let's try a = 0. Then S = (0 - 0 + 9)(9)(0) = 0. Similarly, if a = 3, then S = (27 - 27 + 9)(0)(9) = 0. So maximum must be somewhere in between.Let me try a = 1. Then b = 2. First term: 3(1)² -9(1) +9 = 3 -9 +9 = 3. Second term: (3 -1)² = 4. Third term: 1² = 1. So S = 3*4*1 = 12.If a = 2, b =1. First term: 3(4) -9(2) +9 = 12 -18 +9 = 3. Second term: (3 -2)^2=1. Third term:4. So S = 3*1*4=12. Same as before.What about a = 1.5, b=1.5. Then first term: 3*(2.25) -9*(1.5) +9 = 6.75 -13.5 +9 = 2.25. Second term: (1.5)^2 = 2.25. Third term: 2.25. So S=2.25 * 2.25 * 2.25 ≈ 11.39. Less than 12. So maybe maximum at a=1, b=2 or a=2, b=1.So when c=0, maximum S=12.Similarly, if we set a=0, then S becomes (b² - bc + c²)(c²)(b²). Let’s check if similar maximum occurs here. Let’s take b + c =3.If we set b=1, c=2, then first term: 1 - 2 +4 =3, second term:4, third term:1. So S=3*4*1=12. Similarly, if b=2, c=1, same result. So same maximum. So regardless of which variable we set to zero, the maximum seems to be 12.What if two variables are zero? For example, a=3, b=0, c=0. Then S=(9 -0 +0)(0 -0 +0)(0 -0 +9)=9*0*9=0. So S=0. Not helpful.What about the symmetric case where a = b = c =1. Then each term in S is (1 -1 +1)=1. So S=1*1*1=1. That's way smaller than 12. So clearly, the maximum is not at the symmetric point.Hmm, so when one variable is zero and the other two are 1 and 2, S=12. Maybe that's the maximum. But let's check other cases where none of the variables are zero.Suppose all variables are positive. Let's try a =2, b=1, c=0. Wait, we already considered that. Let's try a case where c is small but not zero. Let's take c=0.1, then a + b=2.9. Let's set a=1.9, b=1. Then compute S.First term: (1.9² -1.9*1 +1²)= (3.61 -1.9 +1)=2.71.Second term: (1² -1*0.1 +0.1²)=1 -0.1 +0.01=0.91.Third term: (0.1² -0.1*1.9 +1.9²)=0.01 -0.19 +3.61=3.43.So S=2.71 *0.91 *3.43 ≈ 8.46. Which is still less than 12. So even with a small c, the value is lower.Alternatively, let's take a=2, b=0.5, c=0.5. Then check S.First term: a² -ab +b²=4 -1 +0.25=3.25.Second term: b² -bc +c²=0.25 -0.25 +0.25=0.25.Third term: c² -ca +a²=0.25 -1 +4=3.25.So S=3.25*0.25*3.25≈ 3.25*0.8125≈2.64. Which is even worse.What if we take a=1.5, b=1.5, c=0. Then S= (1.5² -1.5*1.5 +1.5²)*(1.5²)*(1.5²). Wait, first term is 2.25 -2.25 +2.25=2.25. Then S=2.25*2.25*2.25≈11.39 as before. Still less than 12.Hmm. Maybe 12 is the maximum. But to confirm, let's check another case where none of the variables are 0, but maybe two are equal.Suppose a = b, so 2a + c =3. Let’s set a = b = t, then c=3 -2t.Then S becomes (t² - t² + t²) * (t² - t(3 -2t) + (3 -2t)²) * ((3 -2t)^2 - (3 -2t)t + t²).Simplify each term:First term: (0 + t²) = t².Second term: t² -3t +2t² +9 -12t +4t² = (t² +2t² +4t²) + (-3t -12t) +9 =7t² -15t +9.Third term: (9 -12t +4t²) -3t +2t² + t² = (9 -12t +4t²) -3t +3t² =9 -15t +7t².So S = t²*(7t² -15t +9)*(9 -15t +7t²).Let’s denote u =7t² -15t +9, then S = t²*u*(u) = t²*u².So S = t²*(7t² -15t +9)^2.Now, we can consider this as a function of t. Since a and b must be non-negative, t >=0, and c=3-2t >=0, so 3-2t >=0 => t <=1.5. So t is in [0,1.5].We can try to find maximum of S(t) = t²*(7t² -15t +9)^2 for t in [0,1.5].This might be easier to handle with calculus. Let's compute derivative of S(t):First, let’s set f(t) = t², g(t) = (7t² -15t +9)^2.Then S(t) = f(t)*g(t). The derivative S’(t) = f’(t)g(t) + f(t)g’(t).Compute f’(t) = 2t.Compute g’(t) = 2*(7t² -15t +9)*(14t -15).Therefore, S’(t) = 2t*(7t² -15t +9)^2 + t²*2*(7t² -15t +9)*(14t -15).Factor out 2t*(7t² -15t +9):S’(t) = 2t*(7t² -15t +9)[(7t² -15t +9) + t*(14t -15)].Simplify the expression inside the brackets:(7t² -15t +9) + t*(14t -15) =7t² -15t +9 +14t² -15t=21t² -30t +9.So S’(t) =2t*(7t² -15t +9)*(21t² -30t +9).To find critical points, set S’(t)=0. So solutions are t=0, or 7t² -15t +9=0, or 21t² -30t +9=0.Solve 7t² -15t +9=0:Discriminant D=225 -4*7*9=225 -252= -27 <0. So no real roots. Thus, 7t² -15t +9 is always positive (since coefficient of t² is positive and no real roots). So that term is never zero.Solve 21t² -30t +9=0:Discriminant D=900 -4*21*9=900 -756=144. sqrt(144)=12.t=(30 ±12)/(2*21)= (42)/42=1 or (18)/42=3/7≈0.4286.So critical points at t=1 and t=3/7≈0.4286.Now, check t in [0,1.5]. So critical points at t=0, t=3/7≈0.4286, t=1, and t=1.5 (endpoint). Let's evaluate S(t) at these points.At t=0: S=0.At t=3/7≈0.4286:First compute t=3/7. c=3 -2*(3/7)=3 -6/7=15/7≈2.1429.Compute S(t)= ( (3/7)^2 ) * (7*(9/49) -15*(3/7) +9)^2.First, compute 7*(9/49)=63/49=9/7≈1.2857.Then -15*(3/7)= -45/7≈-6.4286.Add 9: 9/7 -45/7 +63/7= (9 -45 +63)/7=27/7≈3.8571.Thus, S(t)= (9/49) * (27/7)^2= (9/49)*(729/49)= (9*729)/(49^2)=6561/2401≈2.732.At t=1:c=3 -2*1=1.S(t)=1²*(7*1 -15*1 +9)^2=1*(7 -15 +9)^2=1*(1)^2=1.At t=1.5:t=1.5, c=3 -2*1.5=0.S(t)= (1.5)^2*(7*(2.25) -15*(1.5) +9)^2=2.25*(15.75 -22.5 +9)^2=2.25*(2.25)^2=2.25*5.0625≈11.390625.So comparing S(t) at critical points:t=3/7≈0.4286: ≈2.732t=1:1t=1.5:≈11.39So the maximum in this case is at t=1.5, which is when c=0, giving S≈11.39, which is less than 12 as we saw earlier when c=0 and a=1,b=2. So even when considering a = b, the maximum in this case is still lower than 12.Therefore, the maximum seems to occur when one variable is zero and the other two are 1 and 2. Let me confirm again for c=0, a=1, b=2:S=(1² -1*2 +2²)*(2² -2*0 +0²)*(0² -0*1 +1²)= (1 -2 +4)*(4 -0 +0)*(0 -0 +1)=3*4*1=12.Yes, that gives S=12. Similarly, permuting variables gives the same result. So maybe 12 is the maximum.But to be thorough, let's check another case where two variables are equal and the third is different, but not zero. For example, let’s take a = b = t, c =3 -2t, but now t is different. Wait, we already did that. So the maximum in that case was 11.39.Alternatively, let's consider a different case where a ≠ b ≠ c. Let's say a=2, b=1, c=0. We already saw that gives S=12. What if a=2, b=0.9, c=0.1. Let's compute S.First term: a² -ab +b²=4 -1.8 +0.81=3.01.Second term: b² -bc +c²=0.81 -0.09 +0.01=0.73.Third term: c² -ca +a²=0.01 -0.2 +4=3.81.So S=3.01 *0.73 *3.81≈3.01*2.7813≈8.37. Still less than 12.Alternatively, maybe another distribution. Let's take a=1.2, b=1.8, c=0. Then S=(1.2² -1.2*1.8 +1.8²)*(1.8²)*(1.2²). Compute first term: 1.44 -2.16 +3.24=2.52. Then S=2.52*(3.24)*(1.44)=2.52*4.6656≈11.76. Closer to 12, but still less.Wait, actually, if we take a approaching 2 and b approaching1, then S approaches 12. So maybe 12 is indeed the maximum.Wait, but let's check with a=2. Let’s set a=2, b=1, c=0. Then S=3*4*1=12. If we slightly vary a and b such that c is very small but non-zero, does S become slightly more than 12? Let's try a=2.1, b=0.9, c=0. Then a + b =3, but c=0. Wait, c has to be 0 here. If a=2.1, b=0.9, c=0. Then S=(2.1² -2.1*0.9 +0.9²)*(0.9² -0.9*0 +0²)*(0² -0*2.1 +2.1²). Compute each term:First term: 4.41 -1.89 +0.81=3.33.Second term:0.81 -0 +0=0.81.Third term:0 -0 +4.41=4.41.So S=3.33 *0.81 *4.41≈3.33*3.5721≈11.89. Still less than 12.Wait, maybe taking a=1.8, b=1.2, c=0. Then S=(1.8² -1.8*1.2 +1.2²)*(1.2² -1.2*0 +0²)*(0² -0*1.8 +1.8²)= (3.24 -2.16 +1.44)*(1.44)*(3.24)= (2.52)*(1.44)*(3.24). Compute 2.52*1.44=3.6288. Then 3.6288*3.24≈11.76. Still less.Alternatively, taking a=2.5, b=0.5, c=0. Then:First term:6.25 -1.25 +0.25=5.25.Second term:0.25 -0 +0=0.25.Third term:0 -0 +6.25=6.25.Thus S=5.25 *0.25 *6.25=5.25*1.5625≈8.20. Worse.Hmm, so even when varying a and b with c=0, the maximum S occurs at a=1,b=2 or a=2,b=1 with S=12. If I try to set c to a very small positive number, say c=ε, then a + b =3 -ε. Let’s set a=2, b=1 -ε/2, c=ε. Then compute S. When ε approaches 0, S approaches 12. But for any ε>0, the value of S would be slightly less. Let's verify:First term: a² -ab +b²=4 -2*(1 -ε/2) + (1 -ε/2)^2=4 -2 +ε +1 -ε + (ε²)/4=3 - (3ε)/2 + (ε²)/4.Wait, maybe better to compute exactly:First term: a² -ab +b²=4 -2*(1 - ε/2) + (1 - ε/2)^2=4 -2 +ε +1 - ε + (ε²)/4= (4 -2 +1) + (ε - ε) + (ε²)/4=3 + (ε²)/4.Second term: b² - bc +c²=(1 - ε/2)^2 - (1 - ε/2)*ε + ε²=1 -ε + (ε²)/4 -ε + (ε²)/2 + ε²=1 -2ε + (7ε²)/4.Third term: c² - ca +a²=ε² -2ε +4.So when ε approaches 0, first term approaches 3, second term approaches1 -2ε, third term approaches4 -2ε. So S≈3*(1 -2ε)*(4 -2ε)=3*(4 -10ε +4ε²)≈12 -30ε. Which is less than 12. So as ε approaches 0, S approaches12 from below. Therefore, even with small ε>0, S is less than12. So the maximum occurs when c=0, and a=2,b=1, giving S=12.Therefore, after checking various cases, it seems that the maximum value of S is 12, achieved when one variable is 0 and the other two are 1 and 2 (in some order).But to be completely sure, let's check another case where two variables are non-zero and unequal, and the third is non-zero. For example, let's take a=2, b=0.9, c=0.1. Wait, we tried similar before and got S≈8.37. Alternatively, take a=1.5, b=1.4, c=0.1.First term: a² -ab +b²=2.25 -2.1 +1.96=2.11.Second term: b² -bc +c²=1.96 -0.14 +0.01=1.83.Third term: c² -ca +a²=0.01 -0.15 +2.25=2.11.Thus S=2.11*1.83*2.11≈2.11*3.861≈8.15. Still less than12.Alternatively, take a=2.2, b=0.7, c=0.1:First term:4.84 -1.54 +0.49=3.79.Second term:0.49 -0.07 +0.01=0.43.Third term:0.01 -0.22 +4.84=4.63.So S=3.79*0.43*4.63≈3.79*1.9909≈7.55. Worse.Alternatively, take a=1.2, b=1.8, c=0. Then we did that before, getting≈11.76. Close to12 but still less.Alternatively, take a=1.9, b=1.0, c=0.1. First term:3.61 -1.9 +1=2.71. Second term:1 -0.1 +0.01=0.91. Third term:0.01 -0.19 +3.61=3.43. So S≈2.71*0.91*3.43≈8.46. Not good.Alternatively, take a=1.6, b=1.4, c=0.0. Then S=(1.6² -1.6*1.4 +1.4²)*(1.4²)*(1.6²). Compute first term:2.56 -2.24 +1.96=2.28. Then S=2.28*(1.96)*(2.56)=2.28*5.0176≈11.44. Still less than12.So it seems that the maximum occurs when one variable is zero and the other two are 1 and 2, giving S=12. To confirm, perhaps we can use Lagrange multipliers for the original problem with three variables. Let me try setting up the Lagrangian.The function to maximize is S=(a² -ab +b²)(b² -bc +c²)(c² -ca +a²) with constraint a + b + c=3.The Lagrangian is L = (a² -ab +b²)(b² -bc +c²)(c² -ca +a²) - λ(a + b + c -3).Taking partial derivatives with respect to a, b, c, and λ, and setting them to zero. However, this seems extremely complicated due to the product of three terms. The partial derivatives would involve the product rule applied three times, leading to a very messy expression. It might not be practical without more advanced techniques or symmetry.Alternatively, maybe we can use the AM-GM inequality or other inequalities to bound S. However, given the complexity of S as a product of three quadratic terms, it's not straightforward. But perhaps we can analyze each term.Note that each term (x² -xy + y²) is equal to (x² + y² -xy). This expression is known to be equal to (x + y)^2 - 3xy. Since x and y are non-negative, maybe we can find bounds for each term.Alternatively, for any real numbers x, y, we have x² -xy + y² ≥ (x² + y²)/2, by the inequality x² -xy + y² = 3/4 (x - y)^2 + (x + y)^2 /4 ≥ (x + y)^2 /4. Wait, actually:Wait, x² -xy + y² = (x - y/2)^2 + (3y²)/4 ≥ (3/4)(x² + y²) ?Wait, let me check. Let x, y ≥0.x² -xy + y² = x² + y² -xy.Compare with (x² + y²)/2. Then x² + y² -xy - (x² + y²)/2 = (x² + y²)/2 -xy. So whether this is positive depends on x and y.For example, if x = y, then x² + y² -xy = x², and (x² + y²)/2 =x². So equality. If x ≠ y, then it's (x² + y²)/2 -xy = (x - y)^2 /2 ≥0. Therefore, x² -xy + y² ≥ (x² + y²)/2. So each term in S is at least (x² + y²)/2. But that might not help us directly.Alternatively, note that x² -xy + y² ≥ (x + y)^2 /4. Let's check:x² -xy + y² - (x + y)^2 /4 = x² -xy + y² - (x² + 2xy + y²)/4 = (4x² -4xy +4y² -x² -2xy -y²)/4 = (3x² -6xy +3y²)/4 = 3(x² -2xy + y²)/4 = 3(x - y)^2 /4 ≥0.So x² -xy + y² ≥ 3(x - y)^2 /4 + (x + y)^2 /4. Wait, no. Wait, we just showed that x² -xy + y² ≥ (x + y)^2 /4.Thus, each term is at least (x + y)^2 /4. So maybe S ≥ [(a + b)^2 /4][(b + c)^2 /4][(c + a)^2 /4] = [(a + b)(b + c)(c + a)]^2 / (4^3).But since a + b + c =3, (a + b)(b + c)(c + a) = (3 - c)(3 - a)(3 - b). Not sure if this helps. But if we use AM ≥ GM, (a + b)(b + c)(c + a) ≤ ( ( (3 - c) + (3 - a) + (3 - b) ) /3 )^3 = ( (9 - (a + b + c)) /3 )^3 = ( (9 -3)/3 )^3 = (2)^3=8. So (a + b)(b + c)(c + a) ≤8. Thus, S ≥ (8)^2 /64=64/64=1. But this gives a lower bound, not helpful for maximum.Alternatively, since we need an upper bound. Since each term x² -xy + y² ≤ x² + y². So S ≤ (a² + b²)(b² + c²)(c² + a²). Maybe find the maximum of this expression? But even this is not straightforward.Alternatively, note that for fixed a + b + c =3, the maximum of S occurs at the boundary, i.e., when one variable is zero, as we saw. Because when we move from the interior to the boundary (setting one variable to zero), we can increase some terms while decreasing others, but the net effect might be an increase. For example, setting c=0, the term (c² - ca + a²) becomes a², which is larger than if c were positive (since c² -ca + a² = a² -ac +c² ≥a² -ac, and if c>0, then -ac is negative, so a² -ac +c² ≥a² -ac ≥a² -a* something. Not sure. But in our earlier example, when c=0, the third term becomes a², which can be large if a is large. For example, when a=2, c=0, that term is 4. If we take a=2, b=1, c=0, then third term is 4, but if c is small, say c=0.1, then third term is 0.01 -0.2 +4=3.81, which is less than 4. So by setting c=0, we maximize that term. Similarly, the second term when c=0 becomes b², which is 1 when b=1. If c>0, the term (b² - bc +c²) is less than b² +c². For example, setting (a = b) and (c = 3 - 2a) results in a maximum value of approximately 11.39 when (a = 1.5), which is still less than 12.After thorough testing and considering various configurations, the maximum value of (S) is found to be 12 when one variable is zero and the other two are 1 and 2. Thus, the maximum value of (S) is (boxed{12}).]

🔑:Solution: Without loss of generality, let a geq b geq c,then b^{2}-b c+c^{2} leq b^{2}, c^{2}-c a+a^{2} leq a^{2},thus S leq a^{2} b^{2}left(a^{2}-a b+b^{2}right),let a+b=p leq 3, a b=q leqleft(frac{a+b}{2}right)^{2} leqleft(frac{a+b+c}{2}right)^{2}=frac{9}{4}, and p^{2} geq 4 qthus S leq a^{2} b^{2}left(a^{2}-a b+b^{2}right)=q^{2}left(p^{2}-3 qright) leq q^{2}(9-3 q),let f(q)=q^{2}(9-3 q), then f^{prime}(q)=9 q(2-q),if q in[0,2), then f^{prime}(q)>0; if q inleft(2, frac{9}{4}right], then f^{prime}(q)<0,thus max _{q[0.0}]} f(q)=f(2)=12,therefore, the maximum value of s is 12, which is achieved when a=2, b=1, c=0 and its cyclic permutations.

❓:36th Swedish 1996 Problem 2 Find n > 1 so that with stamp denominations n and n+2 it is possible to obtain any value ≥ 2n+2.

🧠:[<think>Okay, so I need to figure out what n > 1 makes it possible to have stamp denominations of n and n+2 such that any value of 2n+2 or higher can be obtained. Hmm, this sounds like a coin problem, but with stamps. The goal is to determine the smallest n where the combination of n and n+2 kronor stamps can cover every amount starting from 2n+2 upwards. First, let me recall that in such problems, usually, the concept of the Frobenius number comes into play. The Frobenius number for two coprime denominations a and b is ab - a - b. But wait, in this case, the denominations are n and n+2. Are they coprime? Let me check. The GCD of n and n+2 would be the same as GCD(n, 2). So if n is even, GCD(n, 2) is 2; if n is odd, GCD(n, 2) is 1. So if n is odd, the denominations are coprime, and if n is even, they have a common divisor of 2.But the problem states that any value ≥ 2n+2 can be obtained. So maybe the Frobenius number (the largest amount that cannot be formed) is 2n+1? If that's the case, then we need the Frobenius number for denominations n and n+2 to be 2n+1. Wait, but if they are coprime, the Frobenius number is n(n+2) - n - (n+2) = n² + 2n - n - n - 2 = n² - 2. Hmm, that's different. So if n and n+2 are coprime, the largest non-representable number is n² - 2. But the problem states that we need to have all numbers ≥ 2n+2 representable. That would mean that the Frobenius number must be ≤ 2n+1. So n² - 2 ≤ 2n + 1. Let's solve this inequality:n² - 2 ≤ 2n + 1 n² - 2n - 3 ≤ 0 Quadratic equation: n² - 2n - 3 = 0 Solutions: n = [2 ± sqrt(4 + 12)] / 2 = [2 ± sqrt(16)] / 2 = [2 ± 4]/2 So n = (2 + 4)/2 = 3 or n = (2 - 4)/2 = -1. Since n >1, n=3 is the solution. So if n=3, then the Frobenius number is 3² - 2 = 9 - 2 =7. But 2n+1 = 7. So that's exactly matching. Therefore, for n=3, the Frobenius number is 7, which is equal to 2*3+1=7, so all numbers starting from 8 (which is 2*3+2=8) can be formed. But in this case, since d=2, and denominations are 4 and 6, so divide by 2: 2 and 3. The Frobenius number for 2 and 3 is 2*3 -2 -3=1. So the answer must be n=3.Wait, but maybe I need to check whether for n=3, all numbers ≥8 can be formed. First, we check the greatest common divisor (GCD) of ( n ) and ( n+2 ). The GCD is 2 if ( n ) is even and 1 if ( n ) is odd. For coprime denominations (when ( n ) is odd), the Frobenius number is given by ( n(n+2) - n - (n+2) = n^2 - 2 ). We need this Frobenius number to be less than or equal to ( 2n + 1 ), ensuring all values ( geq 2n + 2 ) can be formed.Solving the inequality:[n^2 - 2 leq 2n + 1][n^2 - 2n - 3 leq 0]The quadratic equation ( n^2 - 2n - 3 = 0 ) has solutions ( n = 3 ) and ( n = -1 ). 🔑:n=3 Solution 2n+3 - (n+2) = n+1, and 2n+3 < 2(n+2), so the only possible way to get 2n+3 is as n+n+n. Hence n = 3. To show that n=3 works, note that 8 = 3+5, 9 = 3+3+3, 10=5+5. Then by adding additional 3s we can get any larger number. Thanks to Suat Namli 36th Swedish 1996 © John Scholes jscholes@kalva.demon.co.uk 16 January 2004 Last corrected/updated 16 Jan 04

🔑:Answer: 120.Solution: If we add the perimeters of the two squares, we get 100+40=140 cm. This is more than the perimeter of the resulting figure by twice the side of the smaller square. The side of the smaller square is 40: 4=10 cm. Therefore, the answer is 140-20=120 cm.

❓:A B C is a right-angled triangle with the sum of the two legs being s; the height on the hypotenuse is m. Calculate the sides and angles of the triangle.

🧠:[<think>Okay, let's see. So the problem is about a right-angled triangle ABC where the sum of the two legs is s, and the height on the hypotenuse is m. We need to find the sides and angles of the triangle. Hmm, right. Let's start by visualizing the triangle. Since it's right-angled, let's assume the right angle is at point C, so sides AC and BC are the legs, and AB is the hypotenuse. Let me denote the lengths of the legs as a and b, so AC = a, BC = b, and AB = c. The height on the hypotenuse is m. First, the problem states that the sum of the two legs is s, so a + b = s. That's one equation. Then, the height on the hypotenuse is m. I remember that in a right-angled triangle, the height to the hypotenuse can be calculated using the formula: area = (base * height)/2. The area can also be expressed as (a*b)/2, and the base here is the hypotenuse c. So, (a*b)/2 = (c*m)/2. Simplifying, we get a*b = c*m. So that's another equation: a*b = c*m. Also, since it's a right-angled triangle, Pythagoras' theorem applies: a² + b² = c². So now we have three equations:1. a + b = s2. a*b = c*m3. a² + b² = c²Our goal is to find a, b, c, and the angles. Let's see how we can solve these equations. Let's start by trying to express c in terms of a and b, then substitute into the second equation. From equation 3, c = sqrt(a² + b²). Hmm, not sure if that helps directly. Let's think if we can express a² + b² in terms of (a + b)^2. Since (a + b)^2 = a² + 2ab + b², so a² + b² = (a + b)^2 - 2ab. Since we know a + b = s, that's equal to s² - 2ab. Therefore, equation 3 becomes c = sqrt(s² - 2ab). Then equation 2 is a*b = c*m, so substituting c gives a*b = m*sqrt(s² - 2ab). Wait, but sqrt(m² + s²) is greater than m, so m*sqrt(m² + s²) is greater than m², so x is positive. Okay, so ab = m(-m + sqrt(m² + s²)). Hmm, maybe we can factor m out:ab = m( sqrt(m² + s²) - m )Alternatively, we can rationalize sqrt(m² + s²) - m. ❓:10. If integers a, b, c satisfy: 0 leqslant a leqslant 10,0 leqslant b leqslant 10,0 leqslant c leqslant 10,10 leqslant a+b+c leqslant 20, then the number of ordered triples (a, b, c) that satisfy the condition is qquad groups.

🧠:[<think>Okay, let's see. The problem is asking for the number of ordered triples (a, b, c) where each of a, b, c is an integer between 0 and 10 inclusive. Additionally, the sum a + b + c has to be between 10 and 20 inclusive. So, we need to count all possible triples (a, b, c) such that each variable is at least 0 and at most 10, and their sum is at least 10 and at most 20.Hmm. Normally, if there were no upper limits on a, b, c (i.e., they could go up to any number), the problem would be easier. We could use stars and bars or something similar. But since each variable is capped at 10, we have to adjust for those upper bounds. And then also consider the lower bound of the sum being 10.First, let's recall how to count the number of solutions to a + b + c = N where 0 ≤ a, b, c ≤ 10. The standard approach for this kind of problem is inclusion-exclusion. Without any upper limits, the number of non-negative integer solutions to a + b + c = N is C(N + 2, 2). But since each variable can't exceed 10, we need to subtract the cases where one or more variables exceed 10.However, in this problem, we need to do this for all N from 10 to 20 and sum them up. But we are restricted to sum up to 20. Wait, the problem says 10 ≤ sum ≤20. Then add all these numbers together.To compute the number of solutions for a specific S, we can use inclusion-exclusion. The formula for the number of non-negative integer solutions to a + b + c = S without any upper limits is C(S + 2, 2). Then, subtract the cases where any variable exceeds 10. Since the maximum a can be is 10, if we let a' = a - 11, then a' ≥0, and the equation becomes a' + b + c = S - 11. The number of solutions where a >10 is C((S - 11) + 2, 2) if S - 11 ≥0, otherwise 0. Similarly for b and c. Then, we need to add back the cases where two variables exceed 10, since we subtracted them twice. Therefore, the total number of triples with sum outside 10 to20 is 2*S(9). Therefore, the number of triples with sum between 10 and20 is 1331 - 2*220=1331 -440=891. Which confirms the previous result.Therefore, this symmetry argument is a much more straightforward way to compute the answer. Since the number of triples with sum >20 is equal to the number with sum <10, and we already calculated S(9)=220, so 1331 - 220 -220=891.Yes, this is a good check. So, all methods lead to 891. Therefore, I can be confident that the answer is 891.Final AnswerThe number of ordered triples is boxed{891} groups.</think>To find the number of ordered triples ((a, b, c)) such that (0 leqslant a, b, c leqslant 10) and (10 leqslant a + b + c leqslant 20), we can use the principle of inclusion-exclusion and symmetry.1. 🔑:Let's determine the value of lambda at which the area of the triangle cut off by the line connecting two adjacent division points of sides a and b of a polygon is the largest!![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2024_05_02_f60a8259d33d10e0461eg-1.jpg?height=399&width=486&top_left_y=256&top_left_x=814)The area of such a triangle is:t=frac{(a-lambda a) cdot lambda b cdot sin gamma}{2}=(1-lambda) cdot lambda frac{a b sin gamma}{2}We need to examine the sum of such expressions.Since the sum of the expressions frac{a b cdot sin gamma}{2} is constant, it is sufficient to determine the value of lambda at which the expression y=(1-lambda) lambda=-lambda^{2}+lambda is maximal. This function can be represented by a parabola with a vertical axis in the lambda, y coordinate system. This parabola intersects the lambda axis at lambda=1 and lambda=0, and its maximum value is at lambda=0.5.text { ifj. Csonka Pal (Budapest, III. o.) }Remark. 1^{circ}. The maximum value of y=frac{1}{4}-left(1-frac{1}{2}right)^{2} is frac{1}{4}, which is attained where the square term is 0, i.e., at lambda=frac{1}{2}.2^{circ}. The statement is not true for degenerate polygons, because in such cases, some of the triangles involved in the calculation represent a decrease, while others represent an increase in the area of the given polygon. Thus, with the increase of the product lambda(lambda-1), the area of the new polygon may either increase or decrease. For example, if we place 45^{circ} angles at the vertices of a square such that the diagonals are the angle bisectors, we get a star-shaped quadrilateral, for which the polygons created in the above manner all have the same area.
